<div id="review_1794997264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1794997264" />
    <a title="Laszlo Szerdahelyi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13433529-laszlo-szerdahelyi"><img alt="Laszlo Szerdahelyi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548024528p2/13433529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1794997264?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laszlo Szerdahelyi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laszlo Szerdahelyi" href="/user/show/13433529-laszlo-szerdahelyi">Laszlo Szerdahelyi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1794997264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10719564207510743368">Reading Max Stirner today causes a very intense mix of anger,frustration,hopelessness but also serious contemplation, inquiry and reflection on that which has been read, it makes you want to punch the author but also maybe buy him a drink and pick his brain so you can figure out more about his ideas. Considering it was written and published in 1844 and the reaction it caused after, it was a veritable philosophical nuclear bomb thrown in the midst of an ordered and fluid narrative of reason vs fa</span>
  <span id="freeText10719564207510743368" style="display:none">Reading Max Stirner today causes a very intense mix of anger,frustration,hopelessness but also serious contemplation, inquiry and reflection on that which has been read, it makes you want to punch the author but also maybe buy him a drink and pick his brain so you can figure out more about his ideas. Considering it was written and published in 1844 and the reaction it caused after, it was a veritable philosophical nuclear bomb thrown in the midst of an ordered and fluid narrative of reason vs faith, religion vs state etc.<br /><br />In this dense, provocative and yet insightful book, Stirner posits that individuals live under the oppression of abstracts and generalities like: love, freedom, family, Man, State, God, Socialism. Liberalism etc. All of these concepts born out of the thinking of man create invisible ghosts ''spooks'' as he calls them, that haunt our minds, constrict our actions and decisions, make us live our lives according to their precepts and force us to bend the knee to them. In this process, he sees that the individual, the Ego which is used here to refer to one's uniqueness as an individual, is pushed back and losses significance to these notions. Within this Ego, however, Stirner considers that our own true selves lie and we must take ownership of ourselves, recognize that what we do is out of self-interest and our lives should be unchained of anything that hampers our ego. Stirner argues that we must ''storm he heavens'' of religion, political ideologies, dogmas and instead of replacing one notion for another we must loot what we can use and destroy that which is superfluous. Freedom for example is not real absolute freedom but rather the notion that we are permitted by some higher authority to do such and such and that we replace one concept with the other (censorship to freedom of press) Morality and law should not constrict us, for when did we ever consent to their being and why does it benefit us in any way ? Why should we bow to liberalism or Communism for they only perpetuate our slavery by moving from serfdom to citizenry and from citizenry to laborers ?<br /><br />Stirner views the relations between our Ego and all that is material and immaterial in terms of ownership, will and competence. Weather it be a notion or a commodity it should never have property over us, but we should have property over it. We should use it to our desires, for as long as we wish and as long as it fulfills us and discard it once it longer serves us any purpose. Nothing is absolute in terms of notions and nothing can be above us and center our lives as a commodity, lest it become a spook and we become its slave. Our willpower is what determines weather we ''deserve'' to have dominion of things around us. If our will and competence come together in a way that I may preserve and benefit from it for as long as I wish, or for as long as someone with stronger will overcomes me either through wit or physical force.<br /><br />Stirner becomes controversial not only by advocating the stripping of man, the individual , of all notions foreign and outward of him such as law, morality, society etc. but also by how one would attain his egos desires. As far as he is concerned, for example, if you are poor then it is your fault, your will has been such that other stronger than you have dominion over you. If you are capable do whatever satisfies your Ego in changing the dominion over yourself, are you poor and wish to be rid of poverty? Steal, smuggle, kidnap, enslave, murder, disregard any morals and law for they are externalities and use them to your benefit if possible. The world of egoists is a world of total warfare, where one must always be on guard that he be swindled of his power. Stirner follows the maxim of ''Might is Right'' and there is nothing else other than your might (of your will) and no right given to you by law of Men can satisfy your desires. There are passages where Stirner also defends to a certain extent infanticide and incest, although he argues, a man who commits himself to the abstract goal of attaining wealth cannot be free, he is now a slave to a spook, to that of material gain. He argues that in our social relations we for friendships, families, love interests out of self-interest, because it makes us happy, fulfills us or serves a purpose in satisfying ourselves. He elaborates further that although each of us is unique, we should strive to form ''union of egos'' to the extent that such unions satisfy our egoistic needs, of for example, escaping poverty or overthrowing structures that have authority over us . His definition of this is flimsy and brief and offers only a general idea of the concept.<br /><br />Taken in it's entirety Stirner does not necessarily argue for some post-apocalyptic, The Purge-like world, although often he very much sounds like it. Stirners egoist is an individual who ''owns'' himself, has taken the reigns of his life and frees himself from any kind of externalities that are impediments to his enjoyment of life that are imposed on him, for self-enjoyment is the goal of one's life. However, if one becomes slave to one's violence, lust or greed, then he is no longer an egoist, for he is haunted by a spook of which he must be rid of. Stirner rather raises some very serious questions on why and how we do things in our day to day lives and about our values, what principles do we guide ourselves by and do they really benefit us or some other abstract notion or perhaps individual? Stirner offers a sober reminder to ''stay true to ourselves'' to recognize that this truth is within us and to not bend the knee to the tyranny of any collective, to always remember why we act in the ways we do and what made us do so. His packaging and rhetoric can be controversial and in today's world of consumerist individualism some might argue that we already are egoists. Yet, we do not see the spooks of capitalism, consumerism, morality, law etc guiding our lives as we imagine ourselves free and individualistic. <br /><br />His ideas can be understood as Social Darwinism, total self-interest where we are ready to slit the throats of our neighbor if it wills our ego and such become a darling to right wing libertarians such as anarcho-capitalists or rather a harsh reminder of the many layers of abstractions that we have burdened with and the need to unburden ourselves of the things that tie us down and the ways we can take back control over our lives from the dominion of immaterial abstractions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10719564207510743368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_121482406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121482406" />
    <a title="Joseph C" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3160378-joseph-c"><img alt="Joseph C" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263595111p2/3160378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121482406?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph C" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph C" href="/user/show/3160378-joseph-c">Joseph C</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781409961260" href="/book/show/8104308-the-ego-and-its-own">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121482406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7095470737350743551">I got a big Ayn Rand vibe from this book. While Stirner makes several critiques of the State and society which which I could say I generally "like," I cannot agree with his philosophy overall. The Rand commend comes about in the way Stirner makes very repetitive mention of the individual's absolute independence and separation from the rest of society, a position I find untenably anti-social and outright silly. <br/><br/>Stirner raises individuality and selfishness to such a level he essentially comes to b</span>
  <span id="freeText7095470737350743551" style="display:none">I got a big Ayn Rand vibe from this book. While Stirner makes several critiques of the State and society which which I could say I generally "like," I cannot agree with his philosophy overall. The Rand commend comes about in the way Stirner makes very repetitive mention of the individual's absolute independence and separation from the rest of society, a position I find untenably anti-social and outright silly. <br /><br />Stirner raises individuality and selfishness to such a level he essentially comes to be speaking metaphysics rather than any sort of concrete political philosophy. He does so again, and again, and again, to the point where the book could have been tens of pages shorter if such repetitions were cut down. Stirner's attitude is such that the book becomes almost a parody of itself and its ideas.<br /><br />I suppose the book is good if one is looking for an introduction to one of anarchism's (past) main thinkers, but little more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7095470737350743551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_143609217" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143609217" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3746195-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274307381p2/3746195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143609217?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/3746195-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486445816" href="/book/show/257903.The_Ego_and_His_Own">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>

<div id="review_2253833612" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2253833612" />
    <a title="David Sabo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17062410-david-sabo"><img alt="David Sabo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585598744p2/17062410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2253833612?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sabo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sabo" href="/user/show/17062410-david-sabo">David Sabo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2253833612" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4757239815677510592">His words cut like a scalpel into the flesh of the spiritual, the religious, the ethical, the national, the communal... He, like a surgeon, cuts away the fat that are higher essences, however we may call them, and reveals what remains without them- The Ego and Its own. <br/><br/>He is calm and collected in his deconstructive endeavour, but he is also cruel and ruthless, unforgiving and unforgetting. I admire this about Stirner.<br/><br/> "To know and acknowledge essences alone, that is religion its realm is a realm</span>
  <span id="freeText4757239815677510592" style="display:none">His words cut like a scalpel into the flesh of the spiritual, the religious, the ethical, the national, the communal... He, like a surgeon, cuts away the fat that are higher essences, however we may call them, and reveals what remains without them- The Ego and Its own. <br /><br />He is calm and collected in his deconstructive endeavour, but he is also cruel and ruthless, unforgiving and unforgetting. I admire this about Stirner.<br /><br /> "To know and acknowledge essences alone, that is religion its realm is a realm of essences,<br /> spooks and ghosts."<br /><br /> "State, religion and conscience make me a slave and their liberty is my slavery."<br /> <br /> "The fall of people and mankind will invite me to my rise"<br /><br /> "I do not demand any right, neither do I recognize any."<br /><br /> "My power is my propery<br /> My power gives me property<br /> My power am I myself, and through it I am my property" <br /><br /> "Religious heroes of faith are zealous for the "Sacred God", the moral ones for the "Sacred Good"<br /> <br />He recognizes how higher essences "declaw" individuals and how they work on suppressing their egoism. How they exploit us for their benefit. Stirner advocates that we need to seize freedom from them, not long and pine after it- for freedom then becomes an ideal, a spook. In the age of humane liberalism we rely on our human, "natural" rights, that are bestowed upon us the moment we leave our mother's womb. We are entitled to them because we are human and these rights we keep as long as we are "good humans" and "law-abiding citizens." The said right is a reward for our humility. Stirner says to this:<br /><br /> "Freedom belongs to him who knows how to take it, or who does not let it be taken from him,<br /> does not let himself be deprived of it. This is the egoistical "right.""<br /><br />Stirner does not respect any "right", including the right of property nor the right to the "sanctuary of inner peice." He is not tolerant and he resents unconditionally. <br /><br />Stirner values individual power, might and will. He tackles the notion that individuals "should" behave in a certain way and be punished if they do not do so. <br /><br />The book itself was a long and a hard hard journey for me, but it was rewarding beyond descriptive power of my vocabulary. Familiarity with Nietzsche's philosophy made the understanding of this book much easier for me because the two share a lot of views and opinions. <br />The thing I didn't like, and this is a nitpick, is that the book is too lengthy and this is the result of Stirner repeating many of his views throughout the book. This was unnecessary and annoying, but sometimes useful as well.<br /><br />This book is an essential read in my opinion and I give it 4.8/5<br /> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="4757239815677510592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14717206603954001348">hating reading it, but i’m glad i did. that seems to be my feelings towards almost all of the 1800s theory i’ve read. i thought stirner might be different, like the cool uncle of the 19th-century philosophy world, but i was fooled by all the memes i’ve seen. he may be mysterious, but he’s just like the rest of them.<br/><br/>i’m definitely not big brained enough to read this stuff, but these guys could at <i>least</i> try to make their books a little more interesting and comprehensible. sadly, if you want to be </span>
  <span id="freeText14717206603954001348" style="display:none">hating reading it, but i’m glad i did. that seems to be my feelings towards almost all of the 1800s theory i’ve read. i thought stirner might be different, like the cool uncle of the 19th-century philosophy world, but i was fooled by all the memes i’ve seen. he may be mysterious, but he’s just like the rest of them.<br /><br />i’m definitely not big brained enough to read this stuff, but these guys could at <i>least</i> try to make their books a little more interesting and comprehensible. sadly, if you want to be taken seriously when you talk about politics, you have to read every pre-1920 political theory book that has ever existed (especially if it’s over 1000 pages long—the thought of having to read marx’s capital haunts me).<br /><br />i agree with most of stirner’s ideas, but there are some key ones that i can’t get behind. his wit and humour made up for some of the drudgery, and there were several good quotes and passages that resonated with me, but all in all, it was exhausting to read. the next few theory books i pick up will definitely be more recent.<br /><br />anyway, i’m writing this review for andy, who moved this book to the top of my list. it’s safe to say that i will never be taking his recommendations again.</span>

<div id="review_2845652315" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2845652315" />
    <a title="Psalm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19201097-psalm"><img alt="Psalm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491626657p2/19201097.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2845652315?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Psalm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Psalm" href="/user/show/19201097-psalm">Psalm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781620490761" href="/book/show/40195406-the-unique-and-its-property">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2845652315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7898293936268931569">Stirner wasn't a materialist or an idealist. His book speculates on a state of being beyond words, and necessarily beyond philosophy, science or spirit. He wasn't a philosopher. The majority of this text is a satirical slam against Hegelians and others in his peer group. I do tend to associate him with post-structuralism, and to a lesser extent nihilism, but philosophers show their work. Skeptics show their work. Stirner gives you a way of seeing the world and then tells you to do the work. Seek</span>
  <span id="freeText7898293936268931569" style="display:none">Stirner wasn't a materialist or an idealist. His book speculates on a state of being beyond words, and necessarily beyond philosophy, science or spirit. He wasn't a philosopher. The majority of this text is a satirical slam against Hegelians and others in his peer group. I do tend to associate him with post-structuralism, and to a lesser extent nihilism, but philosophers show their work. Skeptics show their work. Stirner gives you a way of seeing the world and then tells you to do the work. Seek out Gustav Landauer for the anarchist articulation of this view. Or seek the Dao. Somewhere between is Stirner, but you'll have to work out for yourself what that means. That's what it means to be an egoist, you know. All you have is what's yours. Make it your property (see:quality). Ask yourself: who do I serve that would have me be so small or dissociated from what I am? What is this in service of? Stirner is cool.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7898293936268931569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1641219983?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Balfour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Balfour" href="/user/show/7944907-david-balfour">David Balfour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7944907-david-balfour?shelf=my_reviews">my_reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1641219983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11267209108615017501">I read a 265-page abridged translation of The Ego and His Own, released in 1971 by Jonathan Cape. The editor claims to have cut down much repetitiveness. Even with so much missing, this is an incredibly thought-provoking book.<br/><br/>Hegel identified that humankind has a tendency to project an unattainable, unchanging beyond, whether spirital, moral, or temporal. We identify with the changeable and yearn painfully to attain this projected beyond:<br/><br/>"Herein lie all the torments, all the struggles of centuri</span>
  <span id="freeText11267209108615017501" style="display:none">I read a 265-page abridged translation of The Ego and His Own, released in 1971 by Jonathan Cape. The editor claims to have cut down much repetitiveness. Even with so much missing, this is an incredibly thought-provoking book.<br /><br />Hegel identified that humankind has a tendency to project an unattainable, unchanging beyond, whether spirital, moral, or temporal. We identify with the changeable and yearn painfully to attain this projected beyond:<br /><br />"Herein lie all the torments, all the struggles of centuries: for it is frightening to be outside oneself, and everyone is outside himself, who has himself as object, without being able to unite this object entirely with himself, and to destroy it as an object, as a substance, and as a resistant thing."<br /><br />Stirner thus tries to bring our consciousness back to the self - to self-consciousness; to reveal the unity of subject and object. This is similar to Hegel, but Stirner approaches it in a different way. Hegel does not advocate the 'destruction' of this object, but its preservation and integration. Destruction is exactly what Stirner wants, however. Hegel does identify this as a possibility, but argues that it leads to subjective anarchy. This is more than enough for Stirner though. He's not interested in the resurrection of Christ, but only his actual existence as a political insurrectionist in Jerusalem. Instead of positing a third term like Hegel, Stirner sticks with the second. He asserts the absolute reality and sole importance of the alienated self; the self as a creative nothing:<br /><br />"The true man does not lie in the future, an object of longing but lies, existent and real, in the present. Whatever and whoever I may be, joyous and suffering, a child or a greybeard, in confidence or doubt, in sleep or in waking, I am it, I am the true man."<br /><br />Stirner holds what man 'is' above what he 'ought' to be, and in doing so opposes himself to Hegel's teleological bent. Instead of man as an abstract concept realising itself in individual men, man is a meaningless abstraction, and individual men are the sole reality: "I live after a calling as little as the flower grows and gives fragrance after a calling." Similarly, Stirner tears down the 'fixed idea' of man when he argues against the Hegelian tendency to personify different thoughts as manifestations of 'geist'. He takes away their validity in and of themselves, refuses to treat thoughts as independent selves, and highlights their sole origin and reality in the individual, human self. <br /><br />Stirner occupies the place of Rameau's Nephew in Hegel's discussion of the world of culture, where utility is the overarching principle. Stirner takes both the physical world and the world of thought to have significance only in and for him. That is, he takes their essence to be utility. Both Hegel and Stirner agree that this leads to a negative and destructive approach to knowledge; an egotistical subjectivism bordering on nihilism. Hegel rejects this, but Stirner revels in it.<br /><br />Despite his shocking rhetoric, Stirner is arguably more compassionate than the high-minded moralist, or the person who fights others in the name of idealistic abstractions like humanity, liberty, equality, or love. Stirner expands upon Hegel's insight that this sort of morality pits a universal ideal against the individual which constitutes its negativity, but also its actuality. So a love of the abstract ideal man becomes the torment of actual, real men once it moves from ideality to actuality through action, as Hegel holds it did during the French Revolution. Stirner fights this tendency by enshrining the actual individual as opposed to some abstract ideal of what he ought to be, and in doing so seems surprisingly compassionate:<br /><br />"Do not call men sinners, and they are not: you alone are the creator of sinners; you, who fancy that you love men are the very one to throw them into the mire of sin, the very one to divide them into vicious and virtuous, into human and inhuman; the very one to befoul them with the slaver of your possessedness; for you love not men, but man. But I tell you, you have never seen a sinner, you have only dreamed of him."<br /><br />Stirner also reiterates the Hegelian insight that when an ideal becomes politicised as the ideology of a group, it inevitably gives rise to hypocrisy and destroys itself. In entering the realm of actual human action and interaction, it enters the realm of negativity. It necessarily becomes concerned with contradictory particulars; despite founding ideals, people necessarily become enmeshed in an unequal power structure of conflicting priorities, and entangled in interpersonal conflict. An abstract ideal or universal thus destroys itself by becoming actual in the human community:<br /><br />"[...] the dissolution of society is intercourse or union. A society does assuredly arise by union too, but only as a fixed idea arises by thought - to wit, by the vanishing of the energy of the thought (the thinking itself, this restless taking back all thoughts that make themselves fast) from the thought. If a union has crystallised into a society, it has ceased to be a coalition; for coalition is an incessant self-uniting; it has become a unitedness, come to a stand-still, degenerated into a fixity; it is - dead as a union, it is the corpse of the union or the coalition, it is - society, community. A striking example of this kind is furnished by the party."<br /><br />No wonder Marx and Engels were so threatened by him!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11267209108615017501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name

    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2wsYHQ2Huapr7CK0/AbwGNM77wmodxd1Qs958evdwbtKXUyq+GJxmcJx23VVym8g73VUYXq5cqr/fGPcKRhVww==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+jjO3pnflSDtd3kBBte40xbmTN8polEoCdVFmNsiOLFrbpppbDpdE0TqgMCvGyfrKqj3t/tsNPe0Zl+1GeesyQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

A Russian Jewish immigrant at the age of 17, she moved by her own efforts from seamstress in a clothing factory to internationally known radical lecturer, writer, editor and friend of the oppressed. This book is a collection of her remarkably penetrating essays, far in advance of their time, originally published by the Mother Earth press which she founded.\n\nIn the first of these essays, Anarchism: What It Really Stands For, she says, &quot;Direct action, having proven effective along economic lines, is equally potent in the environment of the individual.&quot; In Minorities Versus Majorities she holds that social and economic well-being will result only through &quot;the non-compromising determination of intelligent minorities, and not through the mass.&quot; Other pieces deal with The Hypocrisy of Puritanism; Prisons: A Social Crim and Failure; The Psychology of Political Violence—note the relevence of these themes to our own time; The Drama: A Powerful Disseminator of Radical Thought; Patriotism: A Menace to Liberty; and The Tragedy of Woman\'s Emancipation. A biographical sketch by Hippolyte Havel precedes the essays.\n\nAnarchism and Other Essays provides a fascinating look into revolutionary issues at the turn of the century, a prophetic view of the social and economic future, much of which we have seen take place, and above all, a glimpse into the mind of an extraordinary woman: brilliant, provocative, dedicated, passionate, and what used to be called &quot;high-minded.&quot;\n\nUnabridged republication of the 3rd (1917) edition, with a new Introduction by Richard Drinnon. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52198.The_Origin_of_the_Family_Private_Property_and_the_State"><img alt="The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1390348331l/52198._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_52198'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52198.The_Origin_of_the_Family_Private_Property_and_the_State?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2900919.Friedrich_Engels?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Friedrich Engels<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.09 avg rating &mdash; 3,719 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1884\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13700303173122193573\">An eternal being created human society as it is today, and submission to superiors and authority is imposed on the lower classes by divine will. This suggestion, coming from the pulpit, platform and press, has hypnotized the minds of men and proves t<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13700303173122193573\" style=\"display:none\">An eternal being created human society as it is today, and submission to superiors and authority is imposed on the lower classes by divine will. This suggestion, coming from the pulpit, platform and press, has hypnotized the minds of men and proves to be one of the strongest pillars of exploitation. The history of the family dates from 1861, the year of the publication of Bachofen\'s Mutterrecht (maternal law) Engles makes the following propositions: 1. That in the beginning people lived in unrestricted sexual intercourse, which he dubs, not very felicitously, hetaerism. 2. That such an intercourse excludes any absolutely certain means of determining parentage; that consequently descent could only be traced by the female line in compliance with maternal law; and that this was universally practiced by all the nations of antiquity. 3. That consequently women as mothers, being the only well known parents of younger generations, received a high tribute of respect and deference, amounting to a complete women\'s rule (gynaicocracy), according to Bachofen\'s idea. 4. That the transition to monogamy, reserving a certain woman exclusively to one man, implied the violation of the primeval religious law (i.e., practically a violation of the customary right of all other men to the same woman), which violation had to be atoned for its permission purchased by the surrender of the women to the public for a limited time.<\/span>\n  <a Following a logical step-by-step format, it defines in turn the nature of God, the mind, the emotions, human bondage to the emotions, and the power of understanding - moving from a consideration of the eternal, to speculate upon humanity\'s place in the natural order, the nature of freedom and the path to attainable happiness. A powerful work of elegant simplicity, the Ethics is a brilliantly insightful consideration of the possibility of redemption through intense thought and philosophical reflection. The Ethics is presented in the standard translation of the work by Edwin Curley. They chart the course of &quot;true&quot; socialism based on G. W.F. Hegel\'s dialectic, while criticizing the ideas of Bruno Bauer, Max Stirner and Ludwig Feuerbach. Marx expanded his criticism of the latter in his now famous Theses on Feuerbach, found after Marx\'s death and published by Engels in 1888. Introduction to the Critique of Political Economy, also found among the posthumous papers of Marx, is a fragment of an introduction to his main works.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3374165159986413335">Johann Kaspar Schmidt, better known as Max Stirner (the nom de plume he adopted from a schoolyard nickname he had acquired as a child because of his high brow, in German 'Stirn'), was a German philosopher, who ranks as one of the literary grandfathers of nihilism, existentialism, post-modernism and anarchism, especially of individualist anarchism. Stirner's main work is "The Ego and Its Own", also</span>
  <span id="freeText3374165159986413335" style="display:none">Johann Kaspar Schmidt, better known as Max Stirner (the nom de plume he adopted from a schoolyard nickname he had acquired as a child because of his high brow, in German 'Stirn'), was a German philosopher, who ranks as one of the literary grandfathers of nihilism, existentialism, post-modernism and anarchism, especially of individualist anarchism. Stirner's main work is "The Ego and Its Own", also known as "The Ego and His Own" ("Der Einzige und sein Eigentum" in German, which translates literally as "The Only One and his Property"). This work was first published in 1844 in Leipzig, and has since appeared in numerous editions and translations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3374165159986413335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Bentham discusses the weaknesses of the British government as well as the faults within Blackstone\'s treatis<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13557057076690670882\" style=\"display:none\">Jeremy Bentham\'s A Fragment on Government is a critical response to Sir William Blackstone\'s Commentaries on the Laws of England (1765-1769). Bentham discusses the weaknesses of the British government as well as the faults within Blackstone\'s treatises. He argues for the principle of utility, to logically lead his readers to the idea that a legal code should seek the happiness of the largest amount of people. Bentham criticizes Blackstone\'s praise of common law in favor of a more equal social and legal order. JEREMY BENTHAM (1748-1832) was a prominent English social reformer at the turn of the eighteenth century. Although he never practiced law, Bentham was trained as a lawyer and became a vocal critic of the legal system in England. As a political philosopher, he published many works on government and law, such as the Introduction to Principles of Morals and Legislation (1789) and Parliamentary Reform Catechism (1817).<\/span>\n It was written in 1873, in the aftermath of the rise of the German Empire and the clash between Bakunin and Karl Marx in the first <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6241681430602051361\" style=\"display:none\">Statism and Anarchy is a complete English translation of the last work by the great Russian anarchist Michael Bakunin. It was written in 1873, in the aftermath of the rise of the German Empire and the clash between Bakunin and Karl Marx in the first International. Bakunin assesses the strength of a European state system dominated by Bismarck. Then, in the most remarkable part of the book, he assails the Marxist alternative, predicting that a &quot;dictatorship of the proletariat&quot; will in fact be a dictatorship over the proletariat, and will produce a new class of socialist rulers. Instead, he outlines his vision of an anarchist society and identifies the social forces he believes will achieve an ananarchist revolution. Statism and Anarchy had an immediate influence on the &quot;to the people&quot; movement of Russian populism, and Bakunin\'s ideas inspired other anarchist movements. This is the only complete and reliable rendition of Statism and Anarchy in English, and in a lucid introduction Marshall Shatz locates Bakunin in his immediate historical and intellectual context, and assesses the impact of his ideas on the wider development of European radical thought. A guide to further reading and a chronology of events are appended as aids to students encountering Bakunin\'s thought for the first time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6241681430602051361\" href=\"#\"       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1449406'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1449406.Political_Writings?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Political Writings<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/9876.John_Milton?origin=series_works\">John Milton<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.29 avg rating &mdash; 7 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1991\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18157807652749512858\">John Milton was not only the greatest English Renaissance poet but also devoted twenty years to prose writing in the advancement of religious, civil and political liberties. The height of his public career was as chief propagandist to the Commonwealt<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18157807652749512858\" style=\"display:none\">John Milton was not only the greatest English Renaissance poet but also devoted twenty years to prose writing in the advancement of religious, civil and political liberties. The height of his public career was as chief propagandist to the Commonwealth regime which came into being following the execution of King Charles I in 1649. The first of the two complete texts in this volume, The Tenure of Kings and the Magistrates, was easily the most radical justification of the regicide at the time. In the second, A Defence of the People of England, Milton undertook to vindicate the Commonwealth\'s cause to Europe as a whole. They are central to an understanding both of the development of Milton\'s political thought and the climax of the English Revolution itself. This is the first time that fully annotated versions have been published together in one volume, and incorporates a wholly new translation of the Defence. The introduction outlines the complexity of the ideological landscape which Milton had to negotiate, and in particular the points at which he departed radically from his sixteenth-century predecessors. Further aids to students include a full chronology of Milton\'s life and events, a select bibliography and biographies of persons mentioned in the text.<\/span>\n  <a His theory, which builds on Grotius and Hobbes, was immediately recognized as a classic and taken up by writers as diverse as Locke, Hume, Rousseau, and Smith. Over the past twenty years there has been a renaissance of Pufendorf scholarship. On the Duty of Man and Citizen is Pufendorf\'s own epitome of his monumental On the Law of Nature and of Nations, and it served as a basic text in European universities throughout the Enlightenment. This edition has a lucid and historically sensitive translation by Michael Silverthorne, the first since the early twentieth century. James Tully\'s introduction sets the text in its context, summarizes the main arguments, surveys recent literature on Pufendorf, and shows how Pufendorf transformed natural law theory into an       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1769929'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1769929.The_Radical_Reformation?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Radical Reformation<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/405428.Michael_G_Baylor?origin=series_works\">Michael G. Baylor<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.93 avg rating &mdash; 30 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1991\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4881580637880689574\">This book is a collection of writings by early Reformation radicals that illustrates both the diversity and the areas of agreement in their political thinking. The texts are drawn from the period 1521-1527 and center on the major upheaval of those ye<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4881580637880689574\" style=\"display:none\">This book is a collection of writings by early Reformation radicals that illustrates both the diversity and the areas of agreement in their political thinking. The texts are drawn from the period 1521-1527 and center on the major upheaval of those years for German society, the Peasants\' War of 1524-1526.\n\nThe thinkers represented—Muntzer, Karlstadt, Grebel, Hut, Denck, and others—differed on important theological issues. Yet the radicals all rejected the Reformation of the magisterial reformers as serving the interests of society\'s elites. They advocated a strategy of Reformation through direct action from below, a sweeping transformation of society to the benefit of the lay commoner and the local community. \n\nWith the start of the Peasants\' War, radicals divided over the issue of the legitimacy of force. This division shaped the ways in which they confronted the failure of the Peasants\' War as well as the new strategies for survival which they developed in its aftermath. \n\nAppended to the texts are a number of political programs of the Peasants\' War. These documents illustrate ways in which the radicals contributed to the revolution, and how the uprising itself led to greater clarity in the political theory of the radical Reformation.<\/span>\n       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1324141-der-einzige-und-sein-eigenthum">Quotes from The Ego and His Own</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Where the world comes in my way—and it comes in my way everywhere—I consume it to quiet the hunger of my egoism. For me you are nothing but—my food, even as I too am fed upon and turned to use by you. We have only one relation to each other, that of <i>usableness</i>, of utility, of use. We owe <i>each other</i> nothing, for what I seem to owe you I owe at most to myself. If I show you a cheery air in order to cheer you likewise, then your cheeriness is of consequence to <i>me</i>, and my air serves <i>my</i> wish; to a thousand others, whom I do not aim to cheer, I do not show it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/876764-where-the-world-comes-in-my-way-and-it-comes-in">64 likes</a>
